
Hackathon Problem Statement: The "Intelli-Credit" Challenge
Theme: Next-Gen Corporate Credit Appraisal: Bridging the Intelligence Gap
Context
In the Indian corporate lending landscape, credit managers are overwhelmed by a "Data
Paradox"—there is more information than ever, yet it takes weeks to process a single
loan application. Assessing the creditworthiness of a mid-sized Indian corporate
requires stitching together disparate data points:
● Structured Data:
○ GST filin
○ ITRs
○ Bank Statements
● Unstructured Data:
○ Annual Reports
○ Financial Statements
○ Board meeting minutes
○ Rating agency Reports
○ Shareholding pattern
● External Intelligence:
○ News reports on sector trends
○ MCA (Ministry of Corporate Affairs) filin
○ Legal disputes on the e-Courts portal
● Primary Insights:
○ Observations from factory site visits
○ Management interviews (Due Diligence)
The current manual process is slow, prone to human bias, and often misses "early
warning signals" buried in unstructured text.
Problem Statement
Develop an AI-powered Credit Decisioning Engine that automates the end-to-end
preparation of a Comprehensive Credit Appraisal Memo (CAM). The solution must
ingest multi-source data (Databricks), perform deep "web-scale" secondary research, Key Deliverables
Participants must build a prototype that handles the following three pillars:
1. The Data Ingestor (Multi-Format Support) – High latency pipelines
● Unstructured Parsing: Extract key financial commitments and risks from P
annual reports, legal notices, and sanction letters from other banks.
● Structured Synthesis: Automatically cross-leverage GST returns against bank
statements to identify "circular trading" or revenue inflatio
2. The Research Agent (The "Digital Credit Manager")
● Secondary Research: Automatically crawl the web for news related to the
company’s promoters, sector-specific headwinds (e.g., "new RBI regulations 
NBFCs"), and litigation history.
● Primary Insight Integration: Provide a portal for the user (Credit Officer) to in
qualitative notes (e.g., "Factory found operating at 40% capacity"). The AI must
adjust the final risk score based on these nuance
3. The Recommendation Engine
● The CAM Generator: Produce a professional, structured Credit Appraisal Memo
(Word/PDF) summarizing the Five Cs of Credit (Character, Capacity, Capital,
Collateral, and Conditions).
● Decision Logic: Suggest a specific loan amount and interest rate using
transparent, explainable scoring model.
Note: The model must explain why it recommended a rejection or a specific lim
(e.g., "Rejected due to high litigation risk found in secondary research despite
strong GST flows"
Evaluation Criteria
● Extraction Accuracy: How well does the tool extract data from messy, scanned
Indian-context PDFs?
● Research Depth: Does the engine find relevant local news or regulatory fil
that aren't in the provided file
● Explainability: Can the AI "walk the judge through" its logic, or is it a black box? ● Indian Context Sensitivity: Does it understand India-specific nuances li
GSTR-2A vs 3B or CIBIL Commercial reports?
Please note: More details will be provided closer to the hackathon.

Orchestrated comprehensive breakdown of hackathon challenge for beginner understanding
Orchestrated comprehensive breakdown of hackathon challenge for beginner understanding
Understanding the "Intelli-Credit" Hackathon — A Beginner's Deep Dive
Let me break this down step by step, starting from what the problem really is, then how to solve it technically.

🧠 Part 1: Understanding the Problem Deeply
The Real-World Situation
Imagine you're a bank manager in India. A company walks in and says "Give us ₹50 crore loan."

Before you say yes or no, you need to answer:

Are they making real money, or faking it?
Do they have legal troubles?
Is their industry doing well or collapsing?
Are their promoters trustworthy?
Can they actually repay?
Right now, a human credit manager does all of this manually. They read hundreds of pages of documents, call people, Google things, visit factories. It takes weeks and they still miss things.

The hackathon asks you to build an AI system that does this automatically.

The "Data Paradox" Explained Simply
The Irony	Explanation
More data than ever	Banks have GST data, bank statements, court records, news, annual reports...
But still takes weeks	Because a human has to read ALL of it manually
And humans miss things	Early warning signs buried in page 87 of an annual report
The Three Pillars — Plain English
Think of your system as three workers in a factory assembly line:

Raw Documents → [Worker 1: Reader] → [Worker 2: Researcher] → [Worker 3: Report Writer]
🏭 Pillar 1 — The Data Ingestor ("The Reader")
This worker reads ALL the documents fed to it.

What documents?

PDFs → Annual reports, legal notices, sanction letters
GST Returns → Tax filings (GSTR-3B, GSTR-2A)
Bank Statements → Excel/PDF bank transaction history
ITRs → Income Tax Returns
What does it look for?

Key numbers: Revenue, profit, debt, cash flow
Red flags: Is the company filing ₹10 crore in GST but showing ₹50 crore in bank? That's suspicious — called circular trading or revenue inflation
🔑 Key Concept — Circular Trading: Company A pays Company B, Company B pays Company A back. Money goes in circles. The GST filings look huge but no real business is happening. Your AI must catch this by cross-checking GST vs bank statements.

🔍 Pillar 2 — The Research Agent ("The Digital Credit Manager")
This worker goes on the internet and finds things not in the documents.

What it searches for:

News about the company's owners (promoters) — any fraud cases?
Industry news — "Is the steel sector collapsing right now?"
Court cases — Any ongoing litigation?
RBI/regulatory updates — New rules that affect the company?
Plus: A credit officer can type in their own observations, like:

"I visited the factory. Only 40% of machines were running."

The AI must read this note and lower the credit score accordingly. This is called Primary Insight Integration.

📄 Pillar 3 — The Recommendation Engine ("The Report Writer")
This worker takes everything gathered and writes a professional report called a CAM (Credit Appraisal Memo).

The report follows the "5 Cs of Credit":

C	What It Means	Example Question
Character	Is the promoter trustworthy?	Any fraud history? Court cases?
Capacity	Can they repay?	Is cash flow strong enough?
Capital	How much do they own vs owe?	Debt-to-equity ratio
Collateral	What assets back the loan?	Factory land, machinery
Conditions	Is the business environment good?	Industry trends, RBI rules
Finally, it gives a decision: Approve ₹X crore at Y% interest — and explains WHY in plain language.

"Rejected because litigation risk is high (3 court cases found) despite strong GST filings."

💻 Part 2: The Technical Approach (Beginner-Friendly)
Now let's talk about how to actually build this. Think of it like building with LEGO blocks — each block is a technology.

🗺️ The Big Picture Architecture
                    ┌─────────────────────────────────┐
                    │         YOUR AI SYSTEM           │
                    └─────────────────────────────────┘
                              │
         ┌────────────────────┼────────────────────┐
         ▼                    ▼                    ▼
  ┌─────────────┐    ┌──────────────┐    ┌──────────────────┐
  │  INGESTOR   │    │   RESEARCH   │    │  RECOMMENDATION  │
  │  (Reader)   │    │   AGENT      │    │  ENGINE          │
  └─────────────┘    └──────────────┘    └──────────────────┘
         │                    │                    │
         └────────────────────┴────────────────────┘
                              │
                    ┌─────────▼────────┐
                    │   VECTOR DATABASE │
                    │  (Memory Store)   │
                    └─────────────────┘
                              │
                    ┌─────────▼────────┐
                    │    LLM (Claude/  │
                    │    GPT-4/Gemini) │
                    └─────────────────┘
                              │
                    ┌─────────▼────────┐
                    │  CAM REPORT      │
                    │  (Word/PDF)      │
                    └─────────────────┘
🧱 Block 1: The Frontend (What Users See)
Technology: React.js (web app)

Build a simple portal with:

A file upload box (drag & drop PDFs, Excel files)
A text box for credit officer notes ("factory at 40% capacity")
A button that says "Generate CAM Report"
A results page showing the risk score and final report
Think of this as the reception desk of your system.

🧱 Block 2: The Document Reader (PDF/Excel Parsing)
The challenge: Indian PDFs are often scanned images, not real text. You can't just copy-paste from them.

Technologies to use:

Tool	Purpose
PyMuPDF / pdfplumber	Extract text from digital PDFs
Tesseract OCR	Read scanned/image PDFs (converts image → text)
Camelot / Tabula	Extract tables from PDFs
OpenPyXL / Pandas	Read Excel bank statements
Simple flow:

PDF uploaded
    → Is it a scanned image? → YES → Run OCR (Tesseract)
                             → NO  → Extract text directly (PyMuPDF)
    → Extract tables (Camelot)
    → Send clean text to AI
🧱 Block 3: The AI Brain (LLM)
Technology: Claude API (or GPT-4)

This is the "smart" part. Once text is extracted, you send it to an LLM with a prompt like:

"You are an expert Indian credit analyst. Here is the annual report of XYZ Ltd. Extract: Revenue for last 3 years, Total Debt, Net Profit, any mentioned legal risks, and management red flags."

The LLM reads it and gives you structured answers.

Why LLMs are perfect here:

They understand context, not just keywords
They can read unstructured English text and pull out specific data
You can instruct them to follow the 5 Cs framework
🧱 Block 4: The Memory Store (Vector Database)
Technology: ChromaDB or FAISS (both free)

The problem: An annual report can be 300 pages. You can't send all 300 pages to the LLM at once (too expensive, too slow).

The solution — RAG (Retrieval Augmented Generation):

Step 1: Split document into small chunks (500 words each)
Step 2: Convert each chunk into a "vector" (a list of numbers representing meaning)
Step 3: Store all vectors in ChromaDB
Step 4: When you ask "What is the company's debt?", 
        convert the question to a vector too
Step 5: Find the 5 most SIMILAR chunks in the database
Step 6: Send only those 5 chunks to the LLM → Get answer
Think of it like a smart search engine over your documents.

🔑 RAG = Retrieval Augmented Generation — Instead of the AI relying on its training, it retrieves real information from YOUR documents first. This is crucial for accuracy.

🧱 Block 5: The Research Agent (Web Crawler)
Technology: Tavily API or SerpAPI (web search for AI)

This block automatically searches the internet. You give it the company name and it:

Searches Google News for: "XYZ Ltd fraud", "XYZ Ltd promoter news"
Checks MCA (Ministry of Corporate Affairs) portal for filings
Searches eCourts for litigation records
Searches for sector news: "steel industry India 2024 RBI regulations"
Technology flow:

python
search_queries = [
    f"{company_name} fraud news India",
    f"{company_name} promoter background",
    f"{sector} industry outlook India 2024",
    f"{company_name} court cases litigation"
]

for query in search_queries:
    results = tavily_search(query)
    # Send results to LLM to summarize findings
🧱 Block 6: Cross-Verification (The Fraud Detector)
Technology: Python + Pandas

This is the logic that catches circular trading:

python
# Simplified logic
gst_revenue = extract_from_gst_filing()      # e.g., ₹100 crore
bank_inflows = extract_from_bank_statement() # e.g., ₹30 crore

if gst_revenue > bank_inflows * 2:
    flag = "⚠️ SUSPICIOUS: GST revenue significantly 
             higher than actual bank inflows. 
             Possible revenue inflation."
```

**Other cross-checks:**
- GST 2A vs 3B mismatch (input tax credit manipulation)
- ITR profit vs bank statement deposits don't match
- Loan repayments visible in bank statement vs what company claims

---

### 🧱 Block 7: The Scoring Engine

**Technology: Python (rule-based + AI hybrid)**

Build a scoring model with points:
```
FINANCIAL HEALTH (40 points)
  ├── Debt-to-Equity < 2     → +15 pts
  ├── Current Ratio > 1.5    → +15 pts  
  └── Revenue Growth > 10%   → +10 pts

LEGAL/CHARACTER (30 points)
  ├── No court cases found   → +15 pts
  ├── Promoter clean record  → +15 pts
  └── Litigation found       → -20 pts

INDUSTRY CONDITIONS (20 points)
  ├── Sector growing         → +10 pts
  └── No adverse RBI rules   → +10 pts

PRIMARY INSIGHTS (10 points)
  ├── Factory capacity > 70% → +10 pts
  └── Factory < 40% capacity → -15 pts

TOTAL SCORE → 0-100
  80-100: Approve ✅
  60-79:  Approve with conditions ⚠️
  Below 60: Reject ❌
```

---

### 🧱 Block 8: The Report Generator (CAM Document)

**Technology: python-docx (for Word) or ReportLab (for PDF)**

Once all analysis is done, auto-generate a formatted Word document:
```
CREDIT APPRAISAL MEMO
Company: XYZ Limited
Date: March 2026
─────────────────────
1. EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
   Recommendation: APPROVE ₹25 Crore @ 11.5% p.a.
   Risk Score: 74/100

2. CHARACTER ANALYSIS
   [AI-generated paragraphs about promoters]

3. CAPACITY ANALYSIS
   [Revenue, profit, cash flow tables]

4. CAPITAL STRUCTURE
   [Debt-equity breakdown]

5. COLLATERAL
   [Assets offered as security]

6. CONDITIONS
   [Industry trends, regulatory environment]

7. KEY RISK FLAGS
   ⚠️ Factory operating at 40% capacity
   ⚠️ One pending civil dispute found

8. DECISION RATIONALE
   "Approved with reduced limit due to..."
```

| Memory | ChromaDB (RAG) | Handles large documents efficiently |

| Data Analysis | Pandas | Cross-verification of financials |

## 🏆 What Makes a Winning Solution

The judges will look for these 4 things — here's how to nail each:

| Criteria | How to Win |
|---|---|
| **Extraction Accuracy** | Use OCR + show confidence scores; handle Hindi text in docs |
| **Research Depth** | Search MCA, eCourts, RBI website specifically — not just Google |
| **Explainability** | Every score point must show WHY ("Score reduced by 15 pts due to litigation") |
| **Indian Context** | Explicitly handle GSTR-2A vs 3B, mention CIBIL Commercial, use ₹ not $ |
```

Orchestrated comprehensive conceptual overview of three agents sequentially
Orchestrated comprehensive conceptual overview of three agents sequentially
Perfect. Let's go through all 3 agents one by one, conceptually but with enough depth that you understand every design decision. Think of this as the "architect's tour" of the system.

🏗️ Agent 1: The Data Ingestor
What is its one job?
Take a messy pile of documents in different formats and convert them into clean, structured, queryable knowledge that the rest of the system can use.

Garbage in = Garbage out. This agent is the most critical one because if it reads documents wrong, every downstream decision is corrupted.

The Problem with Indian Corporate Documents
Before diving into how it works, you need to understand why this is hard specifically in the Indian context:

Problem	Real Example
Scanned PDFs	A GST notice photographed and uploaded as PDF — it's just an image, no real text
Mixed languages	Annual reports with Hindi annotations in the margins
Inconsistent formats	Every bank has a different bank statement format — HDFC looks nothing like SBI
Bad scans	Rotated pages, blurry stamps, smudged ink
Tables inside PDFs	Financial tables don't extract cleanly — columns get jumbled
Handwritten notes	Site visit observations might be handwritten and scanned
The Ingestor's Internal Pipeline
Think of the ingestor as a post office sorting room. Every document that comes in goes through the same conveyor belt:

Document Arrives
      │
      ▼
┌─────────────┐
│   STEP 1    │  ← Identify: What kind of document is this?
│  Classifier │     (Annual Report / Bank Statement / GST / Legal Notice)
└─────────────┘
      │
      ▼
┌─────────────┐
│   STEP 2    │  ← Is it a real PDF or a scanned image?
│ Format Check│     Real PDF → extract text directly
└─────────────┘     Scanned → run OCR first
      │
      ▼
┌─────────────┐
│   STEP 3    │  ← Pull out the text AND tables separately
│  Extraction │     (Tables need special treatment)
└─────────────┘
      │
      ▼
┌─────────────┐
│   STEP 4    │  ← Clean the mess: remove headers/footers,
│   Cleaning  │     fix encoding issues, remove page numbers
└─────────────┘
      │
      ▼
┌─────────────┐
│   STEP 5    │  ← Send to AI to extract specific data points
│  AI Reading │     (Revenue, Debt, Red flags, Key clauses)
└─────────────┘
      │
      ▼
┌─────────────┐
│   STEP 6    │  ← Store in Vector DB for later retrieval
│   Storage   │
└─────────────┘
Step 1: Document Classification
When a credit officer uploads files, the system needs to automatically figure out what each file is. It does this by:

Filename hints — "GSTR3B_March2024.pdf" is obviously a GST return
First-page reading — Annual reports always start with "Chairman's Letter" or company name + year
Structure recognition — Bank statements have columns like Date, Description, Debit, Credit, Balance
Why does classification matter? Because each document type needs a different extraction strategy. You don't read an annual report the same way you read a bank statement.

Step 2: The OCR Challenge (Most Underrated Problem)
OCR stands for Optical Character Recognition — converting an image of text into actual text.

Here's why this is the hardest part of the whole system:

Imagine someone took a photo of a balance sheet at a slight angle, under tube-light, with a coffee stain on the corner. Your system has to read that perfectly.

The pipeline for scanned documents looks like this:

Scanned PDF
    → Convert each page to a high-resolution image
    → Pre-process the image (straighten, increase contrast, remove noise)
    → Run OCR engine (Tesseract)
    → Post-process (fix common OCR mistakes: "0" vs "O", "1" vs "l")
    → Output clean text
Indian-specific OCR challenges:

Rupee symbol (₹) often misread
Lakh/crore number formatting (1,00,000 not 100,000)
Company names in ALL CAPS often get garbled
Stamp duty stamps overlapping text
Step 3: Table Extraction — The Trickiest Part
Financial data lives in tables. And tables in PDFs are notoriously hard to extract correctly.

Imagine a Profit & Loss table:

| Particulars          | FY 2024  | FY 2023  |
|----------------------|----------|----------|
| Revenue from Ops     | 1,24,500 | 98,200   |
| Other Income         | 2,100    | 1,800    |
| Total Income         | 1,26,600 | 1,00,000 |
When you naively extract a PDF, this becomes: Particulars FY 2024 FY 2023 Revenue from Ops 1,24,500 98,200...

It's a jumbled string. The system needs special table-detection tools that understand the spatial layout of the PDF to reconstruct rows and columns correctly.

Step 4: The AI Reading Layer (The Smart Part)
Once you have clean text, you send it to the LLM with very specific instructions. This is called Structured Extraction.

You're essentially asking the AI:

"Here is the text of an annual report. Your job is to find and return ONLY these specific things: Revenue for last 3 years, EBITDA, Total Debt, any mentions of legal disputes, and any management warnings about the business."

The AI returns a neat, organized response — not a long essay. This is much better than keyword search because the AI understands context. For example:

Keyword search for "revenue": finds every mention of the word "revenue" — including irrelevant ones
AI extraction: understands that "net sales" and "turnover" also mean revenue, and finds the right number
Step 5: The Cross-Verification Engine (Fraud Detection)
This is the most uniquely valuable part of the Ingestor. It's what a junior analyst would spend days doing manually.

GST vs Bank Statement Cross-check
GST Filing says:     Company sold goods worth ₹100 crore this year
Bank Statement says: Total inflows were only ₹40 crore this year

GAP = ₹60 crore → RED FLAG 🚨
Why is this suspicious? Because every sale should eventually result in money coming into the bank. If GST shows massive sales but the bank shows little money, either:

Sales are fake (revenue inflation)
Money is being siphoned before reaching the company bank account
Circular trading (money going in circles between related companies)
GSTR-2A vs GSTR-3B (India-Specific, Very Important for Judges)
This is a uniquely Indian concept and showing you understand it will impress the judges:

GSTR-3B = What the company claims to have paid as input GST (self-reported)
GSTR-2A = What the GST system actually recorded as paid by the company's suppliers
If these two numbers don't match, the company might be claiming fake input tax credits — essentially stealing from the government and inflating their cash position.

ITR vs Financials Cross-check
Income Tax Returns are filed with the government. The company also submits financial statements to the bank. If the profit numbers don't match between the two — massive red flag. Companies sometimes show higher profit to banks (to get loans) and lower profit to the tax department (to pay less tax).

What the Ingestor Outputs
By the end of this agent, you have:

A structured data object for the company:
3 years of key financial ratios
All detected anomalies and flags
Confidence score for each extracted number
A vector database loaded with all document chunks for later retrieval
A flag report: List of all suspicious findings with evidence
🔍 Agent 2: The Research Agent
What is its one job?
Go outside the documents provided and find everything the internet knows about this company, its promoters, its industry, and its legal standing — automatically, in minutes.

A human credit manager does this too, but it takes them 2-3 days of Googling, reading articles, calling contacts. Your AI does it in under 5 minutes.

Why This Agent is a Game-Changer
Documents submitted by the company are self-reported. The company chose what to include. They're not going to put "our promoter was arrested in 2019" in their annual report.

The Research Agent finds things the company doesn't want you to find.

The Agent's Research Playbook
Think of it like a journalist investigating a story. It has a systematic checklist:

🔎 Track 1: Promoter Background Check
Who are the people behind the company? Are they trustworthy?

Searches run automatically:

"[Promoter Name] fraud India"
"[Promoter Name] SEBI action"
"[Promoter Name] ED CBI case"
"[Promoter Name] wilful defaulter"
"[Promoter Name] previous companies"
What it's looking for:

SEBI orders (Securities regulator penalizing them)
ED/CBI cases (financial crime investigations)
Previous companies that went bankrupt under them
Wilful defaulter list (RBI maintains this — companies that could repay but refused to)
🔎 Track 2: Company-Level News
Searches run:

"[Company Name] news 2024"
"[Company Name] plant shutdown"
"[Company Name] layoffs"
"[Company Name] order book"
"[Company Name] rating downgrade"
What it's looking for:

Any recent negative news
Rating agency downgrades (CRISIL, ICRA, CARE downgrading them is a huge red flag)
Factory closures or strikes
Major customer losses
🔎 Track 3: Legal Dispute Search
This is critical and often missed manually.

Sources checked:

eCourts portal (India's court database) — any pending civil or criminal cases
NCLT (National Company Law Tribunal) — any insolvency proceedings
DRT (Debt Recovery Tribunal) — are other banks already trying to recover money from them?
SEBI orders database — any market manipulation cases
A company can look financially healthy but be 6 months away from insolvency because of a case filed at NCLT.

🔎 Track 4: Regulatory & Industry Intelligence
This gives the "Conditions" part of the 5 Cs.

Searches run:

"[Industry sector] India outlook 2024"
"RBI guidelines [sector] 2024"
"[Sector] import duty changes India"
"[Sector] PLI scheme status" (Production Linked Incentive)
Why this matters: A company might be perfectly healthy today, but if the government just announced new import duties that will destroy their margins next quarter — you need to know before lending ₹50 crore.

🔎 Track 5: MCA Filing Check
MCA = Ministry of Corporate Affairs — every company registered in India must file documents here regularly.

The Research Agent checks:

Is the company filing its annual returns on time? (Delays = red flag)
Have there been recent changes in directors? (Sudden director exits = something is wrong)
Has the registered address changed multiple times? (Shell company behavior)
What is the paid-up capital vs what they're claiming?
The Primary Insight Integration (Human + AI Collaboration)
This is the most elegant part of Agent 2. It bridges human judgment with AI analysis.

After the automated research, the credit officer can type in their own observations:

Credit Officer Notes:
"Visited factory on 15th Feb 2025. 
 Production line 2 and 3 were completely idle.
 Workers mentioned they haven't received salaries for 2 months.
 MD was unavailable for meeting — spoke only to CFO."
The AI reads this and does two things:

1. Interprets the severity:

"Idle production lines" → Operating capacity concern
"Unpaid salaries" → Cash flow stress signal
"MD unavailable" → Management transparency concern
2. Adjusts the risk score:

Each observation gets mapped to a risk dimension
Score is reduced with a clear explanation of why
The final CAM report includes these observations as a section called "Primary Due Diligence Findings"
This is important because it means the AI is never fully autonomous — the human's on-the-ground intelligence always overrides. That's what makes judges trust it.

How the Research Agent Thinks (The Loop)
The Research Agent doesn't just do one search and stop. It uses a technique called Agentic Looping:

Start with company name
        │
        ▼
Run initial searches
        │
        ▼
Read results → Find new leads
(e.g., "Article mentions related company XYZ Corp")
        │
        ▼
Run NEW searches on XYZ Corp
        │
        ▼
Keep going until no new leads
        │
        ▼
Compile all findings into a Research Summary
This is what separates a good Research Agent from a basic one. It follows the thread, just like a good investigative journalist would.

What the Research Agent Outputs
Promoter Risk Summary: Clean/Amber/Red with evidence links
Legal Risk Summary: Number of cases, courts, nature of disputes
Industry Outlook: Positive/Neutral/Negative with reasoning
Regulatory Risk: Any new rules that affect this company specifically
Primary Insight Analysis: Credit officer's notes, interpreted and scored
Overall External Risk Score: Out of 100, with item-by-item breakdown
📄 Agent 3: The Recommendation Engine
What is its one job?
Take everything Agents 1 and 2 found, synthesize it into a single coherent view, make a loan recommendation, and write it all up as a professional Credit Appraisal Memo (CAM) — the document an actual bank credit committee would review.

This is the agent that talks to humans. Everything before this was backend intelligence-gathering. This is the output the world sees.

The Two Sub-Components
                 All Data from Agents 1 & 2
                           │
           ┌───────────────┴────────────────┐
           ▼                                ▼
  ┌─────────────────┐             ┌──────────────────────┐
  │  SCORING ENGINE │             │   CAM REPORT WRITER  │
  │  (The Brain)    │             │   (The Pen)          │
  └─────────────────┘             └──────────────────────┘
           │                                │
           ▼                                ▼
   Risk Score + Decision          Professional Word/PDF Document
   Loan Amount + Interest Rate
Sub-Component A: The Scoring Engine
The Philosophy: Transparent, Not a Black Box
The biggest mistake people make is building a complex ML model that gives a score with no explanation. Judges (and real bankers) will reject that immediately. Every point added or deducted must be explainable.

The Scoring Framework
Think of it as a credit scorecard — like a detailed report card with different subjects:

📊 Module 1: Financial Health Score (40 points)
This is purely quantitative — numbers from Agent 1's extraction.

Metric	What it measures	How points are assigned
Debt-to-Equity Ratio	How much debt vs own money	Low D/E → more points; D/E > 3 → negative
Current Ratio	Can they pay short-term bills?	> 1.5 is healthy
DSCR (Debt Service Coverage Ratio)	Can cash flow cover loan repayments?	Most important metric for a banker
Revenue Growth	Is the business growing?	3-year CAGR
EBITDA Margin	Core profitability	Industry-adjusted
Working Capital Cycle	How efficiently they manage cash	Shorter = better
🔑 DSCR is the single most important number in credit. It answers: "For every ₹1 of loan repayment due, how much cash does the company generate?" DSCR of 1.5 means they generate ₹1.50 for every ₹1 owed. Below 1.0 means they literally cannot repay from operations.

👤 Module 2: Character & Integrity Score (25 points)
From Agent 2's research findings.

Finding	Impact
Clean promoter background	+15 points
Promoter on wilful defaulter list	-50 points (immediate rejection trigger)
SEBI/ED action history	-20 points
Previous company default	-15 points
Strong track record (20+ years)	+10 points
Management transparency in interviews	+5 to +10 points
⚖️ Module 3: Legal Risk Score (15 points)
Finding	Impact
No litigation	Full 15 points
Minor civil cases (< ₹1 crore)	-3 points
Major civil cases	-10 points
Criminal cases against promoters	-20 points
NCLT/insolvency proceedings	Immediate flag — review manually
🏭 Module 4: Operational Reality Score (10 points)
This is where the credit officer's site visit notes feed in.

Observation	Impact
Factory at > 80% capacity	+10 points
Factory at 50–80% capacity	+5 points
Factory at < 50% capacity	-10 points
Workers reporting salary delays	-15 points
Management evasive in interviews	-10 points
🌍 Module 5: Industry & External Conditions (10 points)
Condition	Impact
Sector growing, tailwinds present	+10 points
Neutral sector outlook	+5 points
Sector under regulatory pressure	-5 points
Sector in severe distress	-15 points
The Decision Matrix
Once all modules are scored:

Total Score    →    Decision
─────────────────────────────────────────────
85 – 100       →    ✅ APPROVE (Full amount)
70 – 84        →    ✅ APPROVE (With conditions or reduced amount)
55 – 69        →    ⚠️  APPROVE (Significantly reduced + collateral)
40 – 54        →    ❌  REJECT (Refer to higher committee)
Below 40       →    ❌  REJECT (Immediate)

OR if ANY of these triggers fire → AUTO REJECT regardless of score:
  • Wilful defaulter status
  • Active criminal case against promoter
  • DSCR < 1.0 (can't repay from operations)
  • Active NCLT insolvency proceedings
Loan Amount & Interest Rate Logic
If approved, how much and at what rate?

Loan Amount:
  Start with what company requested (e.g., ₹50 crore)
  Apply score-based haircut:
    Score 85+  → 100% of requested amount
    Score 70-84 → 80% of requested amount
    Score 55-69 → 50-60% of requested amount
  Also cap at: DSCR-supported repayment capacity

Interest Rate:
  Base Rate (e.g., MCLR = 8.5%)
  + Risk Premium based on score:
    Score 85+  → +1.5% → Total: 10%
    Score 70-84 → +2.5% → Total: 11%
    Score 55-69 → +4%   → Total: 12.5%
  + Collateral discount: -0.5% if strong collateral provided
Sub-Component B: The CAM Report Writer
This is where the AI's writing ability shines. The CAM is not a data dump — it's a structured narrative that tells the story of the company's creditworthiness to a committee of senior bankers.

The CAM Structure
CREDIT APPRAISAL MEMO
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━

SECTION 1: DEAL SUMMARY (Executive Snapshot)
  • Company Name, Industry, Loan Amount Requested
  • AI Recommendation: APPROVE / REJECT
  • Risk Score: 74/100
  • One-paragraph summary of the whole decision

SECTION 2: CHARACTER (Promoter & Management)
  • Who are the promoters? Background summary
  • Promoter track record
  • Research findings (news, legal history)
  • Management quality assessment from site visit

SECTION 3: CAPACITY (Can They Repay?)
  • 3-year financial trend table
  • DSCR calculation and interpretation
  • Cash flow analysis
  • Working capital cycle

SECTION 4: CAPITAL (Financial Structure)
  • Debt-equity breakdown
  • Existing loan obligations
  • Net worth and reserves

SECTION 5: COLLATERAL (Security Coverage)
  • Assets offered as security
  • Valuation
  • Security coverage ratio

SECTION 6: CONDITIONS (External Environment)
  • Industry outlook
  • Regulatory environment
  • Economic conditions affecting the sector

SECTION 7: RISK FLAGS & MITIGANTS
  • All red/amber flags found, listed clearly
  • For each flag: mitigating factor if any
  Example:
  🚩 Factory at 40% capacity
     ↳ Mitigant: Company has confirmed orders worth 
       ₹30 crore over next 6 months (per management)

SECTION 8: RECOMMENDATION & RATIONALE
  • Final decision in bold
  • Specific loan amount and rate
  • Conditions to be imposed (e.g., quarterly audits)
  • 3–5 bullet points explaining the exact reasoning
  Example:
  "Approved at ₹30 crore (vs ₹50 crore requested) at 
   11.5% p.a. because:
   1. DSCR of 1.4x supports repayment of ₹30 crore
   2. Promoter background is clean with 22 years track record
   3. Reduced from requested amount due to low factory 
      utilization observed during site visit
   4. Steel sector faces headwinds from new import regulations"
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
How the AI Writes This
The CAM writer uses the LLM in section-by-section generation mode:

For each section, it sends the relevant data + a writing instruction:

"You are a senior credit analyst at an Indian bank. Write the 'Capacity' section of a Credit Appraisal Memo for XYZ Ltd. Here is their financial data: [data]. Write in formal banking language. Be specific with numbers. Flag any concerns. Length: 3–4 paragraphs."

Each section is generated separately, then stitched together into one document. This approach:

Keeps each section focused and accurate
Avoids the AI hallucinating numbers from memory
Produces professional, consistent banking language
The Explainability Principle (Most Important for Judges)
The entire Recommendation Engine is built around one core idea: every output must have a traceable source.

Claim in CAM: "Revenue has grown at 18% CAGR over 3 years"
    └─→ Source: Extracted from Annual Report 2024, Page 43, Table 2

Claim in CAM: "Promoter involved in 2019 SEBI action"
    └─→ Source: SEBI order dated 14 March 2019, found via web search

Score deduction: "-15 points for factory underutilization"
    └─→ Source: Credit Officer Note dated 15 Feb 2025
This traceability is what separates your system from a black box. Every banker reading the CAM can ask "how do you know this?" and the system has an answer.

Putting All 3 Agents Together
Here's how they work as one flowing system:

Credit Officer uploads documents + enters company name
                    │
                    ▼
         ┌──────────────────┐
         │   AGENT 1        │
         │   Data Ingestor  │ ← Reads all docs, extracts data,
         │                  │   cross-verifies, flags anomalies
         └──────────────────┘
                    │
          Structured financial data
          + Document knowledge base
                    │
                    ▼
         ┌──────────────────┐
         │   AGENT 2        │
         │ Research Agent   │ ← Searches web, courts, MCA
         │                  │   Integrates officer's site visit notes
         └──────────────────┘
                    │
         External risk intelligence
         + Primary observations
                    │
                    ▼
         ┌──────────────────┐
         │   AGENT 3        │
         │ Recommendation   │ ← Scores everything,
         │ Engine           │   makes decision,
         │                  │   writes CAM report
         └──────────────────┘
                    │
                    ▼
         📄 Credit Appraisal Memo
         ✅/❌ Decision with full reasoning
         📊 Score breakdown
This is the full conceptual picture of all 3 agents. The beauty of this architecture is that each agent is independent but feeds the next — which also means you can build and demo them one at a time if you run short on hackathon time.

Want to now talk about the demo strategy — how to present this to judges in a way that maximizes your score? Or shall we go deeper on any specific agent?

Can we use ealsti search somewhere??

Examined Elasticsearch integration possibilities within system architecture
Examined Elasticsearch integration possibilities within system architecture
Great question! Yes, absolutely — and honestly, Elasticsearch (ES) is a very smart addition to this system. Let me explain where it fits and why.

First, What is Elasticsearch in Simple Terms?
Think of Elasticsearch as a super-powered search database. While a normal database (like MySQL) is great at finding exact matches, Elasticsearch is built for:

Full-text search → Find documents that talk about something, not just exact keyword matches
Fuzzy search → Find "Tata Motars" even if you typed "Tata Motors" wrong
Aggregations → "Show me all companies in the steel sector with revenue > ₹100 crore"
Real-time indexing → Documents are searchable the moment they're stored
Massive scale → Handles millions of documents without slowing down
Where Does It Fit in Your System?
It can slot into multiple agents — here's where it adds the most value:

🏭 Agent 1 — As the Document Store
Right now, the plan was to use a Vector Database (ChromaDB) for storing document chunks. You can use both together — they solve different problems:

                    Documents
                        │
           ┌────────────┴────────────┐
           ▼                         ▼
    Vector Database              Elasticsearch
    (ChromaDB/FAISS)             
           │                         │
    "Find chunks that          "Find documents that
     are semantically           contain exact terms
     similar to this            like 'NCLT', 'wilful
     question"                  defaulter', 'penalty'"
           │                         │
           └────────────┬────────────┘
                        ▼
                   Combined Results
                   sent to LLM
Vector DB = Meaning-based search ("find me anything about debt repayment struggles")

Elasticsearch = Keyword/fact-based search ("find every document that mentions the exact phrase 'SEBI order' or 'NPA'")

Together, this is called Hybrid Search — and it's significantly more accurate than either alone.

🔍 Agent 2 — As the Research Intelligence Store
This is where Elasticsearch really shines uniquely in your system.

Every time the Research Agent crawls the web and finds articles, court records, MCA filings — instead of just summarizing and throwing them away, you index them all into Elasticsearch.

Why? Because now you can do powerful queries like:

Query 1: Find all news articles mentioning 
         [Company Name] AND [fraud OR default OR penalty]

Query 2: Find all legal records where 
         [Promoter Name] appears as defendant

Query 3: Find all companies in steel sector 
         that have been flagged in last 6 months
         (useful if bank is already exposed to the sector)
This turns your Research Agent from a one-time crawler into a growing intelligence database that gets smarter with every company it researches.

📄 Agent 3 — As the Historical Comparison Engine
This is a killer feature that will seriously impress judges.

Every time your system processes a company and generates a CAM, store that profile in Elasticsearch. Now when the next company comes in, you can:

New Company: ABC Steel Ltd, requesting ₹40 crore

Elasticsearch Query:
"Show me all previously assessed companies in the 
 steel sector with similar revenue size (₹80-120 crore)
 and their loan performance"

Result: 
  → XYZ Steel (similar profile) → Loan repaid on time ✅
  → PQR Metals (similar profile) → Became NPA in 18 months ❌

AI uses this as: "Peer Benchmarking Context"
This gives your recommendation engine historical grounding — it's not just theory, it's comparing against real past decisions. Judges will love this because it mimics exactly what experienced credit managers do mentally.

The Full Picture With Elasticsearch
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│              ELASTICSEARCH                   │
│                                             │
│  Index 1: raw_documents                     │
│  (All extracted text, searchable instantly) │
│                                             │
│  Index 2: research_intelligence             │
│  (Web articles, court records, MCA filings) │
│                                             │
│  Index 3: company_profiles                  │
│  (Past CAMs, decisions, loan outcomes)      │
│                                             │
│  Index 4: regulatory_updates               │
│  (RBI circulars, SEBI orders, GST changes)  │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────┘
         ▲                    │
         │ Store              │ Query
         │                    ▼
  Agent 1 indexes       Agent 2 searches
  all document          for past intel
  content               on company/sector
                              │
                              ▼
                        Agent 3 pulls
                        peer benchmarks
                        + regulatory context
One Specific Indian-Context Use Case (For Judges)
Elasticsearch has a feature called Named Entity Recognition (NER) friendly indexing. You can set it up so that every time a document mentions:

A company name → auto-tagged
A person's name → auto-tagged
A court name → auto-tagged
A monetary amount → auto-tagged
So when a credit officer later searches "Show me everything in our database related to Adani Group" — it finds that name across every document, article, and CAM you've ever processed, instantly.

This is basically building a private financial intelligence network for the bank — and Elasticsearch is the backbone of it.

Summary: Where ES Fits
Agent	How Elasticsearch is Used	Value Added
Agent 1	Store & search all extracted document text	Fast, precise retrieval of specific clauses
Agent 2	Index all research findings permanently	Growing intelligence base across companies
Agent 3	Peer benchmarking from historical decisions	Grounds recommendations in real past data
All agents	Regulatory updates index	Always-current RBI/SEBI rule awareness
Adding Elasticsearch also makes your system sound production-ready to judges — it's a real enterprise tool used by actual banks for exactly these kinds of search problems. Want to now connect all of this into a final unified architecture diagram?

LangGraph — The Brain of the Whole System
This is the most important technology choice. Let me explain it deeply.

What is LangGraph?
LangGraph lets you build agents as a state machine graph — think of it like a flowchart where the AI decides which path to take next based on what it found.

Without LangGraph, your system is linear:

Step 1 → Step 2 → Step 3 → Done
With LangGraph, your system is intelligent:

Step 1 → What did we find? 
         If suspicious GST data → trigger deep fraud investigation node
         If clean data → skip to research agent
         If missing documents → ask user for more files
         If NCLT filing found → escalate to senior review flag
The LangGraph State Object
The most important concept. Think of this as the shared memory that all agents read from and write to:

CreditAppraisalState:
{
  # Input
  company_name: "XYZ Steel Ltd"
  documents_uploaded: [list of files]
  officer_notes: "Factory at 40% capacity"
  
  # Agent 1 Outputs
  extracted_financials: {revenue, debt, DSCR, ratios...}
  document_flags: [list of red flags found]
  fraud_indicators: [circular trading, GST mismatch...]
  
  # Agent 2 Outputs  
  promoter_risk: {score, findings, sources}
  legal_findings: [court cases found]
  industry_outlook: {sentiment, key_risks}
  primary_insight_score: -15  ← from officer notes
  
  # Agent 3 Outputs
  final_score: 74
  decision: "APPROVE"
  loan_amount: 30_crore
  interest_rate: 11.5%
  cam_document_path: "/outputs/XYZ_Steel_CAM.docx"
  
  # Control
  current_node: "research_agent"
  errors: []
  needs_human_review: False
}
Every agent reads this state, does its work, and writes its results back. LangGraph manages the flow between agents.

The LangGraph Flow Diagram
                    ┌─────────────┐
                    │    START    │
                    └──────┬──────┘
                           │
                    ┌──────▼──────┐
                    │  Document   │
                    │  Validator  │  ← Are all required docs present?
                    └──────┬──────┘

              ┌────────────┼────────────┐
              │ Missing    │ Complete   │
              ▼            ▼

                                │

              Auto-Reject   Suspicious   Clean
              Trigger       Data Found   Data
                    │           │           │

             │ Flag for │ │  Deep    │ │ Standard │

             │ Rejection│ │  Node    │ │  Agent   │
             └──────────┘ └────┬─────┘ └────┬─────┘

                          │           │           │
                    High Risk    Medium Risk  Low Risk

                      OR borderline           Immediate
                             │               Rejection
                             ▼
                    ┌─────────────────┐
                    │  Human Review   │  ← LangGraph Human-in-the-Loop
                    │  Required Node  │     Senior banker reviews
                    └────────┬────────┘
                             │

                    │   CAM WRITER    │
                    └────────┬────────┘

Why LangGraph's Human-in-the-Loop is a Winning Feature
For borderline cases (score 55–69), the system doesn't auto-decide. Instead it:

Pauses execution
Sends a notification to the senior credit manager
Shows them the full analysis so far
Waits for their input ("Approve with conditions" / "Reject" / "Need more info")
Resumes the graph with their decision baked in
This is exactly how real banks work. Judges will love this because it shows you understand that AI assists humans, it doesn't replace them for high-stakes decisions.

LangChain — The Toolkit Inside Each Agent
If LangGraph is the conductor, LangChain is the instruments. Here's how LangChain is used specifically inside each agent:

Inside Agent 1 — Document Ingestor
LangChain Components Used:

1. Document Loaders
   ├── PyMuPDFLoader → for digital PDFs
   ├── UnstructuredPDFLoader → for scanned/complex PDFs
   ├── CSVLoader → for bank statements in CSV
   └── ExcelLoader → for financial data in Excel

2. Text Splitters
   └── RecursiveCharacterTextSplitter
       (Splits documents into 500-word chunks
        with 50-word overlap so context isn't lost
        between chunks)

3. Embeddings
   └── OpenAIEmbeddings or HuggingFaceEmbeddings
       (Converts text chunks into vectors)

4. Vector Store
   └── ChromaDB
       (Stores all vectors for semantic search)

5. Extraction Chains
   └── LLMChain with structured output parser
       (Asks LLM to extract specific financial data
        and returns it as a Python dictionary,
        not free-form text)
The Extraction Chain Concept:

Instead of asking the LLM "tell me about this company's finances" (which gives an essay), you define an exact schema and ask the LLM to fill it in:

Schema defined:
{
  revenue_fy24: float,
  revenue_fy23: float,
  revenue_fy22: float,
  total_debt: float,
  ebitda_margin: float,
  legal_mentions: list[string],
  management_risks: list[string]
}

LangChain's structured output parser forces 
the LLM to return ONLY this format.
No hallucinations. No essays. Just data.
Inside Agent 2 — Research Agent
LangChain Components Used:

1. Tools (what the agent can "do")
   ├── TavilySearchTool → web search
   ├── ElasticsearchTool → search your own index
   └── WikipediaLoader → background on companies/sectors

2. LangChain Agents (ReAct pattern)
   The agent follows: Reason → Act → Observe → Repeat
   
   Thought: "I need to find court cases for XYZ Ltd"
   Action: Search eCourts database
   Observation: "Found 2 cases in Bombay High Court"
   Thought: "I should also check if promoter is named"
   Action: Search for "XYZ Ltd promoter name court"
   Observation: "Promoter Rajesh Shah named in civil case"
   Final Answer: [structured risk report]

3. Memory
   └── ConversationSummaryMemory
       (Keeps track of what's been researched 
        so agent doesn't repeat searches)
The ReAct Agent Pattern — Why It's Powerful:

A regular program follows instructions blindly. A ReAct agent thinks between each action. This means if it finds something unexpected mid-research (like discovering a related shell company), it can pivot and investigate that new lead — just like a human investigator would.

Inside Agent 3 — Recommendation Engine
LangChain Components Used:

1. RAG Chain (Retrieval Augmented Generation)
   Query: "What is the company's DSCR?"
   → Search ChromaDB for relevant chunks
   → Retrieve top 5 most relevant passages
   → Send to LLM with instruction to answer from context only
   → Get accurate, grounded answer (not hallucinated)

2. LLMChain with Custom Prompts
   Each section of the CAM is a separate chain:
   └── CharacterSectionChain
   └── CapacitySectionChain
   └── CapitalSectionChain
   └── CollateralSectionChain
   └── ConditionsSectionChain
   └── DecisionRationaleChain

3. Sequential Chain
   Chains run in sequence, each feeding into the next
   so the Decision Rationale references everything
   written in earlier sections
Elasticsearch — Deeper Integration
Now with LangChain + LangGraph in place, here's how Elasticsearch integrates at every level:

The 4 Indices (Think of as 4 Specialized Databases)
Index 1: document_store
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
Fields: company_id, document_type, page_number, 
        content, extracted_entities, date_processed
Use: Full-text search across all ingested documents
Special: Auto-tags company names, amounts, 
         dates, court names using NER

Index 2: research_intelligence  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
Fields: company_id, source_url, article_date,
        sentiment_score, risk_category, content_summary
Use: Agent 2 stores all web research here permanently
Special: Grows over time → second company in same 
         sector benefits from first company's research

Index 3: company_profiles
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
Fields: company_id, sector, revenue_band, final_score,
        decision, loan_amount, outcome_after_12_months
Use: Historical benchmarking for Agent 3
Special: After 1 year, bank updates "outcome" field
         (repaid / NPA / restructured) → 
         system learns what good companies look like

Index 4: regulatory_watchlist
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
Fields: regulation_date, issuing_body, affected_sectors,
        risk_level, summary, full_text
Use: Automatically populated by a daily crawler
     that reads RBI, SEBI, MCA websites
Special: When any company is assessed, this index
         is checked for recent regulations affecting
         their sector
Elasticsearch + ChromaDB Together (Hybrid Search)
User/Agent asks: "What are the debt obligations of XYZ Ltd?"

                    Query
                      │
          ┌───────────┴────────────┐
          ▼                        ▼
   Elasticsearch              ChromaDB
   BM25 keyword search    Semantic vector search
   finds: exact matches    finds: contextually 
   for "debt", "loan",     similar passages even
   "obligation", "NCD"     if different words used
          │                        │
          └───────────┬────────────┘
                      ▼
              Reciprocal Rank Fusion
              (Algorithm that merges 
               both result sets smartly)
                      │
                      ▼
            Top 5 most relevant passages
            sent to LLM for final answer
This hybrid approach is what the industry calls best-in-class RAG and significantly reduces hallucinations.

The Supporting Technologies
LangSmith — Observability (Secret Weapon for Demo)
LangSmith is LangChain's monitoring platform. Every single LLM call, every agent decision, every tool use is automatically logged and visualized.

Why this wins the hackathon: During your demo, you can literally show judges a dashboard that says:

CAM Generation for XYZ Steel Ltd
Total time: 4 minutes 23 seconds
LLM calls made: 47
Documents processed: 12
Web searches performed: 23
Tokens used: 84,200

Agent 2 Research Path:
  ✅ Search 1: "XYZ Steel promoter background" → 8 results
  ✅ Search 2: "Rajesh Shah SEBI" → Found 1 relevant order
  ✅ Search 3: "XYZ Steel court cases" → 2 cases found
  ✅ Search 4: "steel sector India 2024 outlook" → 5 articles
  
Decision Trace:
  Score started at 100
  -12 pts: D/E ratio above industry average
  -15 pts: Factory at 40% capacity (officer note)
  -8 pts:  One pending civil case
  +5 pts:  Clean promoter background
  Final: 70 → APPROVE with conditions
Judges can literally click through every decision and see the AI's reasoning. That's explainability at the highest level.

Redis — Two Critical Roles
Role 1: Task Queue When a credit officer uploads documents, the processing takes 3–5 minutes. You don't want the browser to freeze. Instead:

User uploads docs → 
API creates a task → 
Pushes to Redis queue → 
Returns task_id immediately to user → 
UI shows progress bar → 
Celery worker picks up task → 
Runs LangGraph pipeline → 
Updates progress in Redis → 
UI polls Redis for updates → 
Done! CAM ready notification
Role 2: Caching If the same company was researched 2 weeks ago, don't re-run all web searches. Cache the research results in Redis with a 30-day expiry. Saves time, saves API costs.

Unstructured.io — The Document Parsing Upgrade
This is a dedicated library built specifically for parsing complex documents. It handles things standard PDF parsers can't:

Multi-column layouts (common in annual reports)
Tables that span multiple pages
Headers and footers (should be excluded from analysis)
Figures and chart captions
Footnotes (often contain critical risk disclosures)
Scanned PDFs with mixed orientations
It integrates directly with LangChain as a document loader — one-line upgrade that dramatically improves extraction accuracy.

Databricks — The Enterprise Data Layer
The problem statement specifically mentions Databricks. Here's where it fits:

Raw Data Sources (GST API, Bank Feeds, MCA)
              │
              ▼
         Databricks
         (Delta Lake)
         ├── Bronze Layer: Raw data as-is
         ├── Silver Layer: Cleaned & validated
         └── Gold Layer: Business-ready tables
                    │
                    ▼
              FastAPI Backend
              reads structured
              financial data
              directly from
              Gold tables
For the hackathon, you can simulate this by having pre-processed CSV/JSON files that represent what Databricks Gold tables would output. Mention Databricks in your architecture diagram — it shows you read the problem statement carefully.

Scalability Built In
Here's how the architecture handles scale — important to mention to judges:

Horizontal Scaling
Multiple credit officers using system simultaneously?
  → Multiple Celery workers running in parallel
  → Each company assessment is an independent task
  → Redis queue distributes work automatically
  → No bottleneck
Multi-Tenancy (Multiple Banks)
Different banks using the same system?
  → Each bank gets isolated Elasticsearch indices
  → Each bank's historical data stays private
  → Shared LLM layer (cost efficiency)
  → Separate LangSmith projects per bank
Cost Optimization
LLM calls are expensive. Three strategies:
  1. Cache repeated research (Redis, 30-day TTL)
  2. Use smaller LLM (Haiku) for simple extractions,
     larger LLM (Sonnet) only for CAM writing
  3. Batch similar companies together 
     (e.g., all steel companies in one run 
      shares industry research)

Architected cross-document correlation and latency optimization strategies
Architected cross-document correlation and latency optimization strategies
Great — these are the two hardest real-world engineering problems in this entire system. Let's go deep on both. This is where your project separates from every other team's.

Part 1: Connecting the Dots Across Pages & Documents
First, Understand Why This is Hard
Let's make the problem concrete with a real example:

Annual Report — Page 200:
"The company has entered into an offtake agreement 
 with Greenfield Energy Pvt Ltd for 40% of production"

Financial Statement — Page 555:
"Revenue concentration risk: Single customer accounts 
 for 38% of total revenue"

Board Meeting Minutes — Page 12:
"Discussion on Greenfield Energy's delayed payments 
 — receivables outstanding for 180+ days"

News Article (External):
"Greenfield Energy Pvt Ltd files for insolvency 
 at NCLT Mumbai — March 2025"
A human reading page 555 in isolation sees a revenue concentration risk.
A human reading page 200 in isolation sees a normal business agreement.
A human reading the news sees an unrelated insolvency story.

Only a human who connects all four dots realizes:

This company is about to lose 38% of its revenue because its biggest customer just went bankrupt.

That is a loan-killing insight buried across 4 different places. Standard RAG completely misses this. Here's why:

Why Standard RAG Fails at This
Standard RAG workflow:

Question: "What are the key risks?"
    ↓
Search vector DB for chunks similar to "key risks"
    ↓
Returns: The 5 chunks that are semantically 
         closest to "key risks"
    ↓
LLM reads those 5 chunks and answers

PROBLEM:
The Greenfield Energy connection never surfaces
because no single chunk contains all the pieces.
Each chunk, in isolation, looks ordinary.
The danger is in the RELATIONSHIP between them.
The Solution: Knowledge Graph + GraphRAG
This is the most cutting-edge technique in enterprise AI right now. Microsoft released GraphRAG in 2024 specifically to solve this problem.

What is a Knowledge Graph?
Instead of storing documents as chunks of text, you store them as a web of connected entities and relationships:

Standard RAG stores:
"Chunk 47: The company has entered into an offtake 
 agreement with Greenfield Energy for 40% of production"
[just a blob of text]

Knowledge Graph stores:
(XYZ Steel) --[HAS_CUSTOMER]--> (Greenfield Energy)
(XYZ Steel) --[REVENUE_DEPENDENCY: 38%]--> (Greenfield Energy)
(Greenfield Energy) --[HAS_STATUS]--> (NCLT Insolvency)
(XYZ Steel) --[HAS_RECEIVABLE: 180 days]--> (Greenfield Energy)
(XYZ Steel) --[RISK: Revenue Concentration]--> (Single Customer)

Now the system can TRAVERSE these connections:
"Follow all relationships involving Greenfield Energy
 → Find XYZ Steel is connected
 → Find the revenue dependency
 → Find the receivable delay
 → Find the insolvency news
 → ALERT: Critical compound risk detected"
Think of it like a spider web — pulling one thread moves connected threads. Standard RAG stores information in isolated bubbles. A knowledge graph stores information as a living, connected network.

The Full "Connect the Dots" Architecture
                    ALL DOCUMENTS + WEB RESEARCH
                              │
                              ▼
                    ┌─────────────────────┐
                    │   ENTITY EXTRACTOR  │
                    │   (LangChain +      │
                    │    Claude)          │
                    └─────────┬───────────┘
                              │
         Extracts all named entities and relationships:
         People, Companies, Amounts, Dates, 
         Agreements, Risks, Legal Cases, Assets
                              │
                              ▼
                    ┌─────────────────────┐
                    │    NEO4J            │
                    │  Knowledge Graph    │  ← Stores the web
                    │                    │    of connections
                    └─────────┬───────────┘
                              │
              ┌───────────────┼───────────────┐
              ▼               ▼               ▼
       Entity Search   Path Finding    Community Detection
       "Find all nodes  "How is XYZ     "Which cluster of
        connected to    Steel connected  entities form a
        Greenfield      to this NCLT     suspicious pattern
        Energy"         case?"           together?"
              │               │               │
              └───────────────┼───────────────┘
                              ▼
                    ┌─────────────────────┐
                    │    GRAPHRAG         │
                    │  (Microsoft)        │  ← Reasons ACROSS
                    │                    │    the graph
                    └─────────┬───────────┘
                              │
                              ▼
                    ┌─────────────────────┐
                    │  COMPOUND INSIGHT   │
                    │  GENERATOR          │
                    │                    │
                    │ "XYZ Steel has      │
                    │  critical exposure: │
                    │  38% revenue from   │
                    │  Greenfield Energy  │
                    │  which filed NCLT   │
                    │  insolvency.        │
                    │  Receivables 180+   │
                    │  days outstanding.  │
                    │  Sources: pg 200,   │
                    │  pg 555, news       │
                    │  article March 25"  │
                    └─────────────────────┘
The Three Layers of Intelligence
Layer 1: Entity Extraction (Building the Graph)
When every document is ingested, before storing in ChromaDB, you run an entity extraction pass:

Input text:
"The company has entered into an offtake agreement 
 with Greenfield Energy Pvt Ltd for 40% of production
 under a 5-year contract signed in FY2022"

Extracted entities and relationships:
{
  entities: [
    {name: "XYZ Steel Ltd", type: "COMPANY"},
    {name: "Greenfield Energy Pvt Ltd", type: "COMPANY"},
    {name: "FY2022", type: "DATE"},
    {name: "40%", type: "PERCENTAGE"},
    {name: "5 years", type: "DURATION"}
  ],
  relationships: [
    {
      from: "XYZ Steel Ltd",
      to: "Greenfield Energy Pvt Ltd", 
      type: "OFFTAKE_AGREEMENT",
      properties: {
        percentage: 40,
        contract_start: "FY2022",
        duration: "5 years",
        source_page: 200,
        source_document: "Annual Report 2024"
      }
    }
  ]
}
This runs for every chunk, every document, every web article. Slowly, a rich graph of the company's entire world gets built.

Layer 2: Graph Traversal (Following the Threads)
Neo4j lets you query the graph using a language called Cypher. Think of it like SQL but for relationships:

"Find me all entities connected to this company 
 that have ANY negative signal"

Graph Query Logic:
START at XYZ Steel
FOLLOW all relationships outward (2-3 hops)
FILTER nodes that have properties like:
  - status = "NCLT" or "defaulter" or "fraud"
  - sentiment = "negative"
  - risk_flag = true
RETURN the full path from XYZ Steel to the risk

Result:
XYZ Steel → [HAS_CUSTOMER] → Greenfield Energy 
→ [HAS_STATUS] → NCLT Insolvency
PATH LENGTH: 2 hops
RISK: CRITICAL
Multi-hop reasoning is what makes this powerful. It can find connections that are 3, 4, or 5 steps removed — things no human would think to check.

Layer 3: Community Detection (Pattern Recognition)
This is the most advanced capability. Neo4j can automatically detect clusters of entities that are suspiciously interconnected.

Real use case — Shell Company Detection:

Company submits: They have 5 major suppliers

Graph analysis reveals:
  Supplier A → Director: Ram Sharma
  Supplier B → Director: Ram Sharma  
  Supplier C → Director: Priya Sharma (wife of Ram Sharma)
  Supplier D → Registered address: same as XYZ Steel office
  Supplier E → Incorporated 6 months before first transaction

Graph Pattern Alert:
"CIRCULAR TRADING SUSPECTED
 4 out of 5 major suppliers are connected to 
 company insiders. This cluster of entities 
 shows hallmarks of related-party transactions
 designed to inflate revenue."
No human would catch this without spending days cross-referencing MCA filings. The graph catches it automatically because all these entities got indexed when documents were ingested.

Complete Tech for "Connect the Dots"
Technology	Role	Why This One
Neo4j	Knowledge graph database	Industry standard, has built-in graph algorithms
Microsoft GraphRAG	Reasoning across the graph	Specifically built for this multi-hop problem
spaCy / GLiNER	Entity extraction from text	Fast, accurate for Indian company names & legal terms
LangChain + Neo4j	Bridge between LLM and graph	Native integration, queries graph in natural language
Cypher Query Engine	Traversing relationships	Neo4j's query language, like SQL for graphs
Part 2: High Latency Pipeline Problems
Map All the Latency Bottlenecks First
Before solving, let's identify every single place where time is lost:

Total time for one CAM = sum of all these delays:

1. PDF Upload & Storage           → 5-10 seconds
2. OCR on scanned documents       → 30-120 seconds per document
3. Text chunking & embedding      → 20-40 seconds (12 documents)
4. Entity extraction (per chunk)  → LLM call per chunk = SLOW
5. Knowledge graph building       → Neo4j write operations
6. Web searches (Research Agent)  → 20-30 searches × 2 sec each
7. LLM calls for analysis         → 5-10 sec each × 40+ calls
8. CAM section writing            → 6 sections × 8 sec each
9. Document generation            → 5 seconds
                                    ─────────────────
Total naive sequential time:      → 15-25 MINUTES
Target time:                      → Under 5 MINUTES
That's a 5x speed improvement needed. Here's how you get it:

Problem 1: Sequential Processing (The Biggest Killer)
The Problem
Current (Sequential — SLOW):
Process Document 1 → wait → 
Process Document 2 → wait →
Process Document 3 → wait → ... → Done
[Total: 12 documents × 45 seconds = 9 minutes just for ingestion]
The Solution: Parallel Processing with Celery
Parallel Processing (FAST):

Document 1 ──────────────────────────────────────────▶ Done
Document 2 ──────────────────────────────────────────▶ Done  
Document 3 ──────────────────────────────────────────▶ Done  [All at same time]
Document 4 ──────────────────────────────────────────▶ Done
...
Document 12 ─────────────────────────────────────────▶ Done
[Total: 45 seconds regardless of how many documents]
How it works with Celery:

Upload triggers:
    ├── Celery Task: Process Annual Report    ──▶ Worker 1
    ├── Celery Task: Process Bank Statement   ──▶ Worker 2
    ├── Celery Task: Process GST Returns      ──▶ Worker 3
    └── Celery Task: Process Legal Notice     ──▶ Worker 4

All workers run SIMULTANEOUSLY
Results stored in Redis as each finishes
LangGraph waits for ALL tasks to complete
Then proceeds to Research Agent
Problem 2: OCR Latency (The Worst Offender)
The Problem
Tesseract OCR on a 100-page scanned PDF takes 2–4 minutes. You can't make the user wait.

Solution: Three-Pronged Attack
Prong 1: Pre-processing Queue As soon as a file is uploaded, OCR starts immediately in the background — even before the user clicks "Analyze":

User uploads PDF
    ↓
Instant response: "File received ✅"
    ↓ (simultaneously, in background)
OCR worker starts processing immediately
    ↓
By the time user fills in company details
and clicks Analyze → OCR is already done
Prong 2: Page-Level Parallelism Don't OCR 100 pages sequentially. Split the PDF into individual pages and OCR all pages at the same time:

100-page PDF
    ↓
Split into 100 individual page images
    ↓
Send all 100 to 10 parallel OCR workers
    ↓
Each worker handles 10 pages simultaneously
    ↓
Time: 100 pages ÷ 10 workers = 10x faster
Prong 3: Smart OCR Routing Not all pages need OCR. First check if the PDF has real text:

Page analysis:
  Has selectable text? → Skip OCR, use direct extraction (fast)
  Is it an image?      → Run OCR (slow, but necessary)

Result: Only 30% of pages typically need OCR
        70% are processed instantly
Problem 3: LLM Call Explosion
The Problem
Entity extraction requires an LLM call per chunk. 12 documents × 50 chunks each = 600 LLM calls. At 3 seconds each = 30 minutes.

Solution: Batch + Model Tiering
Strategy 1: Batching

Instead of one LLM call per chunk, send 10 chunks in one call:

Naive approach:
  Call 1: "Extract entities from chunk 1"  → 3 sec
  Call 2: "Extract entities from chunk 2"  → 3 sec
  ...600 calls...                          → 30 min

Batched approach:
  Call 1: "Extract entities from ALL of these 
           10 chunks, return results for each"  → 5 sec
  ...60 calls...                               → 5 min
Strategy 2: Model Tiering (Big Cost + Speed Win)

Task Type                          Model Used
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Simple extraction (find numbers)   Claude Haiku    ← Fast + Cheap
Cross-verification logic           Claude Haiku    ← Fast + Cheap
Entity recognition                 Claude Haiku    ← Fast + Cheap
Research summarization             Claude Sonnet   ← Balanced
CAM section writing                Claude Sonnet   ← Quality needed
Final decision rationale           Claude Sonnet   ← Most important
Haiku is 5x faster and 10x cheaper than Sonnet. Use it for the bulk work. Reserve Sonnet for where quality matters.

Strategy 3: Streaming for Perceived Speed

For CAM writing, don't wait for the entire section to be generated. Stream it word by word to the UI:

Without streaming:
  User waits 45 seconds → Then entire CAM appears at once
  [Feels slow and broken]

With streaming:
  Words appear immediately, like ChatGPT
  User sees progress in real time
  [Feels fast and alive]
Problem 4: Knowledge Graph Write Latency
The Problem
Building Neo4j graph while processing documents adds overhead. Every entity and relationship needs to be written to the database.

Solution: Async Graph Building
Document Processing Pipeline:

Stage 1: Extract text (blocking — needed immediately)
Stage 2: Generate embeddings → store in ChromaDB (blocking)
Stage 3: Extract entities → queue for Neo4j (NON-BLOCKING)

The LangGraph pipeline moves forward using ChromaDB.
Neo4j graph is built ASYNCHRONOUSLY in the background.
By the time Agent 3 needs graph insights, 
the graph is already populated.

This overlaps Neo4j writes with LLM processing time.
Net additional latency: ~0 seconds.
Problem 5: Web Search Latency (Research Agent)
The Problem
The Research Agent needs to run 20–30 searches. At 2 seconds each, that's 60 seconds minimum — and they're currently running one after another.

Solution: Parallel Search + Intelligent Caching
Parallel Search:

Sequential (SLOW):
Search 1: "XYZ Steel promoter"     → 2 sec
Search 2: "XYZ Steel fraud"        → 2 sec
Search 3: "XYZ Steel court cases"  → 2 sec
...20 searches...                  → 40 seconds

Parallel (FAST):
All 20 searches fire simultaneously
Each gets its own async worker
Total time = time of SLOWEST single search = ~3 seconds
Intelligent Caching with Redis:

Before running any search, check Redis cache:
  Key: "research:XYZ_Steel:promoter_background"
  TTL: 7 days

If cache HIT → return instantly (0 seconds)
If cache MISS → run search, store result in Redis

Result: Second time any company is researched
        → Research Agent takes ~5 seconds instead of 60
        → Entire sector research is reused across companies
Pre-warming (Proactive Caching):

When credit officer uploads documents:
  → System extracts company name immediately
  → Silently starts background research in parallel
     with document processing
  → By the time Agent 1 finishes → Agent 2 results 
     are already partially ready
Problem 6: The Embedding Bottleneck
The Problem
Generating vector embeddings for thousands of text chunks is slow with external APIs (OpenAI, etc.) because each chunk requires a network call.

Solution: Local Embedding Model
Run embeddings locally instead of via API:

API-based embeddings (slow):
  Chunk → HTTP request → OpenAI servers → response → 0.5 sec × 600 chunks = 5 min

Local embeddings (fast):
  Using: sentence-transformers/all-MiniLM-L6-v2
  Chunk → local model → embedding → 0.02 sec × 600 chunks = 12 seconds
  
  25x faster. Zero API cost. Works offline.
  Quality is 90% as good for retrieval tasks.
The Complete Latency Solution Map
PROBLEM                 SOLUTION                    TIME SAVED
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
Sequential documents    Celery parallel workers     8 min → 45 sec
OCR on scanned PDFs     Page-level parallelism      3 min → 30 sec
                        + pre-processing queue
LLM call explosion      Batching + model tiering    30 min → 3 min
Knowledge graph build   Async background writing    2 min → 0 min
Web search latency      Parallel + Redis cache      60 sec → 5 sec
Embedding generation    Local sentence-transformers 5 min → 12 sec
CAM writing feel        Streaming output            Feels 5x faster
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
TOTAL                                               ~22 min → ~4 min
